BUTLER’S POINT AT MARION TO BE DEVELOPED

Ap? 30° 22.

* When the Butler’s Point Association completes the development of the pot
lint, if present specifications are carried out, the neck will look like this. Marion
town officials expect that although only about $300,000 is being spent at this ti’
me in the development, about a million dollar valuation will be added to the
three and one-half at which the town is now valued.
In the plan the club house is at the tip of the point with the tennis courts a
nd the hotel, which will eventually be constructed, behind it. The small basin is
to be dredged out until it will have sufficient depth to provide an anchorage for
| the Beverly Yacht Club fleet and visiting clubs. The 18-hole golf course with
: | many natural hazards will extend over much of the remainder of the point, an
| d most of the course will be within sight of blue water.
The golf course and building has been leased to the Kittansett Club, which
is composed chiefly of members of the Beverly Yacht Club, Along the Sippican
harbor side there are house lots, which eventually will be sold.
Work on the development has already begun and a gang of about 100 men i
s on the job, There is also a ten-ton tractor, which is now engaged in pulling
up big trees, roots and all.

The Last Survivor’: oo
of the Ship Roman!

Thomas Luce.’

THE LAST SURVIVOR.

DEATH OF 'THOMAS LUCE, MEM=
BER OF THE ROMAN'S CREW.

Whaling Merchant and Years Ago
Was Interested in the Cooperage
Business——Successful Seeker After

Gold in California,

| Thomas’ ‘Luce, the last survivor of
the crew of the vessel Roman, a well
known whaling agent in this city fez
| years, and one of the Seekers for gold
jin California in '49, died at his home,
550 County street, Sunday.. Mr: Luce
had been ill for about three years, but
had net been confined to his bed un=
til three weeks before his death,
Captain Luce was born in the West
ern islands, January 6, 1827.° He came
to this country when a boy and settled
in Falmouth. In 1844 he sailed for
a voyage to the northwest coast. on
the ship Roman... Fifty years after
Captain Luce entertained the eight

surviyors of the erew at his home in
County street, Since that day, Noyem-
ber 2, 1894, all.of the then remaining
eight have died, and Captain Duce
was the last. The sailing of the ship
Roman was a long remembered event
in the minds of the men, and at the
time of the celebration of the 50th
anniversary a most novel reunion took
place.

in 1849 Captain Luce went to Gali-
fornia with the thousands of gold
seekers now so often referred to as

successful. When he returned. from
California he started a cdaperage

terests, and in 1888 he entered th
business as an agent. He fitted and
owned as many as 25 vessels, He
continued in business until six yearsm@y
ago, when he retired.

Mr, Luce was married twice, the
first time to Hannah B. Luce, the mar-
riage taking place in Falmouth. She
died in 1879. His second marriage |}
was to Lydia BE, Payne of Auburn, Ne
Y. The second wife died three weeks
ago. Charles T, Luce, a son, and 4ived
grandchildren survive. 1

Mr, Luce was an extensive travel
for the past 25 years. Every winter}
was passed in Florida, and much. of]
his time was spent in Europe. He
jobservyed his 80th birthday anniver-]
sary in Rome, Italy. .

The funeral services will be held)
\from his home Thursday afternoon
lat 2 o’clock. Burial will be in Rural
cemetery.

"HARKS BACK TO. *
WHALING DAYS

~_

Grinnell House Built in 1830 Ee
One of City’s Vener- |
able Mansions:

It Served as Headquarters for
the Red Cross During
the World War

Approaching the century mark is
the venerable Grinnell mansion which
crowns a vast sweep of green lawn on

the west side of County street. It is
now the home of Mr. and Mrs. Law-
rence Grinnell, who gave it up to the
Red Cross as New Bedford headquar-
ters during the war, whence the name,
“The Red Cross House,” by which
many persons know it. It was built in
the 1830's by Joseph Grinnell, whose
name is associated with the indus-
trial development of New Bedford,
notably the establishment of the Wam-
sutta Mills.

The Grinnell, house belongs to the
period when whaling was still a
proud industry of this city. As one
enters the blue-tiled hall a stained
glass window above the stair landing, —
representing a whaling bark speeding§
with all sails set before a windy blue
sky, is the first object to attract at-
tention. ‘The hall, like all the halls
of houses of this . period, ~ pierces
through the center of the house. Blue
wall coverings in a Pompeiian design}
to harmonize with the tiled floor,
white woodwork relieved by a carved
oak leaf rosette in each corner of the
door frames, and severely plain silver
knobbed mahogany doors carry out
an austere scheme.

Austerity Vanishes.

The impression of austerity vanishes
at the first glimpse into the living}
rooms. The music room is bright in}
its scheme of cream walls and Gop-
pery gold hangings. The heavy carved
gold frames of portraits and land-}
scapes, gilt candelabra and chande-
liers, and the daintily colored French’
upholstery of the furniture contrast
effectively with the handsome man-|
tel of solid black and black and}
chandeliers, and the daintily colored!

| French upholstery of the furniture j
| contrast effectively with the handsome jg
mantel of solid black and black and
gold variegated marble. An open
door into a green shuttered conserva- }
tory filled with fragrant blooming
plants adds a final touch of color to
this inviting. room.

A wide doorway set between double
fluted columns throws the music room |
and living room into one. In the liv- |
ing room cream walls and carpet and
comfortable chairs and a divan of}
rich blue create a satisfying color}
scheme, with the mahogany of tables,
desk and doors. The architect who
designed this massive stone house con-
ceived the idea of rounding the end |
of the living room on each side of the j
broad doorway so as to make room:
for cupboards. The mahogany doors
of these closets, smaller but otherwise ]
reproducing the other doors in the }
house, are curved to fit the curve of |
the wall. z

The dining room is hospitably long, |
with a fine fireplace at one end. Be-
| tween the three windows are twin wall’

cupboards of ivory enamelled wendy
pery like buffets with overhanging |)
' chain closets reaching almost to the

lofty ceiling. A tremendous oriental}
rug in which rose tones predominate)
gives color to the room, A fine Chip-

_pendale sideboard, marquetry chairs, |
polished dining table and drop-leaf
serving table, contribute to produce

an effect of simple elegance,

\e spacious dining room and a|
old Pashioncd bookeases, and an}

Another corner of the reception room.’ The old mahogany secretary holds a fine shell collection. The stair landing which app
in both pictures has a counterpart in the adjoining dining room, ,

Felix Gonnevjlle.

health during the last year and had
been confined to his home since Feb-
ruary. He was a sufferer from
Bright's disease.

Born in St. Guillaume, Canada, Nov.
4, 1843, Mr. Gonneville remained in
that city where he was engaged in
farming until 1884, when he moved
to Woonsocket. After .making his
home there for twelve years, he
moved to New Bedford, and from that}
time on made his home in this city. }
He married Miss Hermine Chausse
of St. Elizabeth, Quebec, in 1866.

Made Day Memorable,

The most memorable event in Mr.

life was the observance

when the couple were surrounded by
their eleven children, some of whom
had come from distant points to make
the anniversary a memorable one,
During that day both Mr. and Mrs.
Gonneville attended. high mass at the
Sacred’ Heart church, at. which their
son, Rev, P. H. Gonnewville, was cele-
brant. The couple entertained with
uw banquet in the afternoon and a re-
ception in the. evening.

Public bequests amounting of $17,-
|600 were made by the late Rodolphus
| Beetle, who died recently, for although

he left no will apportioning his large
estate there have been found memo-
randa made by him several years ago

{ in which he gives a list of those whom
be wished to benefit by his property.
A number of the local charitable or-
| canizations were remembered by Mr.
Beetle with individual sums, and he
named one sum to be distributed
among other city charities.

The list of the public bequests made
| by Mr. Beetle is as follows:

BEETLE

Following Mr. Beetle’s death on the
9th of this month, after a very brief
ilIness, every effort was made to find
a will if he had made one, but among
all his papers no such document could
be found. Accordingly application
was made last week by Henry 8.
Hutchinson and J. Henry Herring, his
sons-in-law, for appointment by the
probate court as administrators of
his estate.

It was not until after this that
among a number of other papers were
found two memoranda in which Mr.
Beetle had given lists of people and
organizations which he wished to re-
member. The papers were in no form
whatever, although at the top of the
first one he had made a statement
that he wished to make the above
bequests.

One paper was dated March 2, 1894,
but this list had been changed several
times, apparently, for figures had been
ferased and written over, and names
had been crossed out where the bene-
ficiary had died, as if Mr. Beetle had
desired his bequest to go to the per-
sons alone, and not to their heirs. |

Folded inside this first paper was}

1902, but without the heading of the
previous one, as.if he had expected
it to be understood that the figures
given in his revised memorandum were
to be considered as bequests. This
second memorandum contained sev-
eral more names that the first, and
the amounts from the original paper.
had been in every case considerably
increased, Besides the Ist of public
hequests given above, a number of In-}
diyiduats are remembered with vari-
ous sums. 3

In the list made by Mr. Beetie he

}gave the sum of $7,000 for a tree bed |

at St. Luke's hospital, for that was the}
sum required at the time the memo-
randum was made, Since that time,
however, the trustees have increased
the amount required to $10,000, and
'Mr. Beetle’s family have decided to
| inerease his bequest to that sum, in or-
| der to fulfill his wish for a free bed
lat the hospital.

Mr. Beetle had béen known by in-
\timate friends during his lifetime as
a man of great benevolence, although ;
his charities were always conducted
in such a quiet and unostentatious
manner that few outside the benefici- |
aries themselves knew of his gifts. He.
had beén much interested in the local
charitable organizations, and the find-
ing of these memoranda in which hej

HIGHER RANK

| i
hin Co A. Pearson, For-

mer,Commander of Prince-
ton Reserve Corps Is Now
Lieutenant Colonel

Major John A.) Pearson, in charge
of the Reserve Officers Training Corps
at the New Bedford High School re-
ceived official notification y erday
afternoon from the War Department
jat Washington of his promotion to
the rank of Lieutenant Colonel

Lieutenant Colonel Pearson has
been on duty in this ¢ity as instruc-
tor at the High School since- duty
of last year when he relieved Colonel
D. T. EB. Casteel.

Lieutenant Colonel Pearson was
born in Tennessee and was an ape
pointee from fhat state to West Point,
graduating from the academy in 1901.)

Major John A, Pearson,

He served as second lieut
the lith cavalry in the
1901-1904. He later we
first lieutenant in the Seventh Cavalry,
jreturning to the United with
|that regiment in 1907. Im April, 1907;
he was promoted to the rank of cap-
tain,
From March, 1916, to February,
}1917, he served with the Mexican
Punitive Expedition and ‘at various
times during his military career has
been the winner of every gold medal
given in both army and national
matches for rifle shooting. Shortly
after the outbreak of the war with
Germany he was appointed. Tecruit-
ing officer for the Boston district and
in March, 1918, was sent to the Wal-
ter Reed Hospital at Washington, D.
c., for treatment for injuriés ceived
hited the campaign ,in ‘Wexico in

promoted to

1916,

Headed Princeton S. A, T. OG.
| After being discharged from the
hospital, he was appointed command=
ling officer at Princeton University,
holding that office from April to De-
cember, 1918. Leaving this’ come=
mand, he was a gned to duty with
the General Staff in Washington from
January to April, 1919. Duritig the
World War he was promoted from
the rank of captain to that of ter:
porary major. of cavalry, lNeutenan
colonel and then to colonel

While on duty at Camp Mea
Maryland, on Sept. 30,1919. he w
notified by the»-War Department
his retirement from active servi
with the rank of captain. The eau)
of his being retired was due to woun
received in line of duty while at Me
ico, He remained on the inacti
list until July of last year when

jwelcome delegation to Dr.

T. H. Lee, replying to The Standard
in response to the invitation extended
\the Chinese students to assist in wel-
coming Minister Dr. Sze, wrote, “We
will join the other groups in wel-
coming Minister Sze in the coming
August. We do this on the basis of
international friendship. In the mean-
time, we wish to offer our hearty
ithanks for your kindness in inform-
ing us of the same.”

Little Edwin Tobey Morrill is nearly as large as most of Ms playmates
who are twice his age, but he won’t reach his second birthday until the end of
August. He can say ’most anything he wants to, and’can really and truly
whistle. He’s not afraid of any animals he has ever seen, and is especially.
deyoted to real dogs and pieture-book elephants. His favorite sport is auto-
mobiling, when he can see all the sights, including birds, squirrels, hens and
rabbits.. Edwin is the son of Mr. and Mrs. True ©, Morrill, and will ¢ome +o
live in New Bedford July 1, when his daddy assumes the superintendency of
the city schools,

} AMONG THE FIRST GAS CARS HERE

Arthur Shill. Edward Shill.

The distinction of making four college years in three and a half goes to the
younger of two brothers, Arthur E. Shill, of 240 Rounds street, who, with
his brother Edward, received his, degree of A. B. at the Harvard College com-

4} mencement exercises last Tuesday. Arthur was graduated from the New Bed-
ford High School with the class of June, 1917, being the youngest member of
the class. Because he was able to boast of only 16 years he was not permitted)
to enter Harvard until September, 1918. His brother Edward was graduated
from the New: Bedford High School in June, 1916, and was a member of the
lelass. of 1920 ati Harvard, but owing to service rendered during the World
war was not abl is degree until last Tyesda:

HE. EARNED IT IN 24 HOURS

This happy-looking young man is none other than James Gillick, of 7%
James street, the boy who earned a Columbia Motobike within 24 hours of the
time he started out.

James-the Enterprising. signed up in the Standard-Mereury subserip
tion contest last Friday, obtaining the necessary subscription blanks and. iW
structions at The Standard office. Saturday, he had turned-in the 25 new suly
seribers necessary to earn him the Motobike, by Monday they had ‘beqit®
checked up and found allright and that evening he was riding his brand new
Berkshire blue Motobike.

James showed some speed in acquiring his hieyele, averaging more thay
a new subseriber for every half hour he worked if he worked the full 12 hours
If James did it, isn’t it probable that other boys and girls, too,-ean do it!
When it’s as easy as that, to obtain. a new bicyele,—and -one of the finest
made, af that,—it. surely seems as though every hoy and girl in New Bail
ford ought to be able to go out and earn. one, ‘

And the fun isn’t all over for James yet, either. He and the other
winners of Motobikes are all going to be priests of the management out at the
Cycledrome, Thursday evening, Aug. 5, to participate, in’a big bicycle parade g
around the flat of the track with prizes for the best decorated Motobike and to)
wateb the races from the reserved section.

RY WASHERS ARE NOW BEING MADE GETTING BIRDS

EGGS HIS HOBBY

Walter B/Savary Has Collec-
tion of 1500 Specimens

from All Over the Coun-
try

Thanks to the hobby of Walter
B. Savary of Bast Wareham, either
Wareham High School or the Marion
Natural History Society may event-
ually be the richer by an excellent
collection of birds’ eggs, gathered
from the greater part of the United
States in the course of more than
half a, century.

Mr. Savary is now in Maine, add-
ing to his collection.

He has between 1200 and 1500
specimens including eggs of about
260 varieties. There are about 775
species of birds north of the Mexi-
can border, according to the latest
check list of the American Orni
thologists’ Union.

This collection has been a labor
of love with him, and he-has taken
small count of the money expended

Fe sows eight of 1e@ new Per Ws ) <
Works. Already orders fo1 RN ras a Ne the assembly room of the present plant bef
: to tirn-them out. At the right one of fee Re ie Ga ied in faster than the present peat Hanah upon his trips for the time spent in
ns up-to give an opportunity to life out the Faye ag OER, showing how the outer shell of the collecting, or “blowing” the eggs, Or
s a®view of the machine shop with the drills in ernudes containing the clothes, identifying and filing them away. He
nner ¢ylinders, and the metal working poe Sy ie center foreground for boring the wooden has been in California three times,
his ben h while Pagel ne and in the background. At the and in the South, the ‘Southwest
, Je ¢ etly in_front of him is the planer and Alaska and Canada.

Travelled Far and Wide,

For his travels, far and wide, he

has procured a Federal permit 19

take birds’ eggs, which: can only be

to Al al ade er ae a recom ae
| of a nationally-known ornithologist.

aska and- Back on Bet His present trip to Maine is his first

HE to that state, although he has been

in all the other New England states:

His collection ranges from the tiny
humming bird's egg, about .45 of an
inch long, to the egg of the Wider
duck, nearly four inches long.

“The study of birds is one of the
most interesting subjects in the
world,” he says, “particularly pird
architecture. The artistry of the
birds passes ° understanding. Some
birds sit and turn round and round =
to get the sides of their nests eye. &
Some birds build nests: with parts oft
snake. skins. The oriole strings
leaves together and sews them UP to
make a pouch. Some birds puild
very strong nests, and some are 4°
big as/four or fiye feet in diameter.”

Paid Dollar for Egg.

A collection of eggs such as Mr.
Savary has gathered cannot be made
without. considerable expense, and
each egg that he has filed away has)
cost him varying~ amounts, He fis
ures that the eggs from California,
of which there are about 500 have
cost him a dollar apiece.

Studies of the Edmund Wood House

The brown shingled mansion of’Hdmund Wood, at Ash and Hawthorn streets, half hidden behind a stone wall and a hedge of A jwealth of books, beautiful paintings, and. a most hospitable fireplace of soft tan brick framed in mellow oak give a setting for
artistically planted shrubbery, reveals unsuspected spaciousness. ‘The dining room and living room, adjoining end to end, make possible the sindller art objects’ whieh are found in the living room. No photograph can. do justice to the dignity of the dining room, panelled
hospitality on a magnificent scale. Both have beamed ceilings and open by glass doors leaded in diamond shaped panes upon a sunny high inj black oak, above which is a frieze of golden dolphins on a rich red ground. The shining mahogany of the furniture, the gleam-
suite comprising study, sun room, and plant room, which together have a sweep of 60 feet, overlooking a grassy terrace. ing silyer on the sideboard, and the polished brass above the mantel are set off by the somber simplicity of their background.

The sun room is a'place of comfortable wicker and cushions, its warm stained woodwork contrasting agreeably

| with the fresh green of rose vines, wisteria, and shrubbery that invites to the tiled terrace above the lawn. \At one end

| ithe plant room lures with its ferny depths and the sound of water trickling from a stone fountain set in the wall; at

\the other the well stocked bookeases of Mr. Wood’s study appearing through the glass partition tempt one’s footsteps in
the opposite direction.“ = : —= <2

Edward A. Cunniff.

Inventor of the new automatic
loom which is expected to work a
revolution in the cotton manufactur- |}
ing industry, and will run indefinitely
jwithout stopping except for broken
warp threads. Mr. Cunniff has a long
list of patents on spinning and we
ing machinery and was the origin-
ator of the warp stop motion such
hs is now used on the automatic
looms.

MAY HONOR HERO

This is Private Walter Goulart of
the Machine Gun Battalion of the
827th Infantry who was killed in
action in France during the Meuse-
Argonne offensive on Hill 180 on Oct,
7, 1918, while placing™his machine
gun in position. He is the son of
Antone and Frances. P, Goulart of
142 Fair street.

Members of South End clubs and
prominent South-end merchants and
citizens have petitioned the city goy-
ernment to name officially the square
formed by the intersection of Rivet
and Bolton streets ‘Walter .Goulart
Square” in honor of Private Goulart,
friend and comrade of many of the
petitioners.

If the petitions are granted, the
petitioners agree to beautify the
square and to ereet a tablet there
bearing Private Goulart’s citation
for bravery “as an. example to the
youths in that section and as a fitting
testimonial.”

ERATIONS IN ONE FAMILY GATHERED

—Photo by Joseph A, Drouin

This photograph shows four generations of the Fonteneau family of Ox-
ford, Fairhaven. From left to right they are: front row, Mrs. William Vigent,
William Vigent, Mrs. Arthur Boulet, Omer Brodeur, Rev. N. H. G. Gaulin of
Woonsocket, R, I., who is visiting his nephews, who are members of this fam-
ily group; Mrs. Omer Brodeur, Alfred Guilmette, and Mrs. Alfred Guilmette;
second row, Leo Boulet, Miss Idella Boulet, Oliva Boulet, Miss Alice Brodeur,
Leo Brodeur, Aleide Brodeur, Armand Guilmette, Albert Guilmette; last row,
Mrs. Wilfred Fonteneau, Wilfred Fonteneau, Alfred Fonteneau, Mrs. Alfred
Fonteneau, Albert. Fonteneau, Miss Ida Fonteneau, William Fonteneau and
Mrs. William Fontenea 1.

A quarter of a century's active work
in New Bedford was preceded by a
decade of service in Somerville, where

friend of Dwight &..’Moody, Mrs. Keyes
Cc. A

first became interested in Y. M.

of the Somerville Auxiliary. Coming

Caster assumed

Keyes turned her executive ability to
excellent purpose, and during the sev

en years of her presidency of the}
Women's Auxiliary brought nearly
$8,000 to the association, In 1913 she
resigned to befome matron of the
Women's Rescue and Relief Associa~|
tion. Her earnest efforts in behalf}
of the 254 women and girls who
sought her motherly advice and help
| lmake the three years of her stay there

munity.” When il) health forced her}
in 1916°to leave this work, for a much
needed rest with her mother and sister
in Wells, Maine, her loss was severely
felt by this and other local charities
with which she had labored, She was
an active worker in the North Congre-
gational Church.

Mrs. Catherine: Haran, of 90 Walden street, announces the engagement
lof her daughter, Miss Kathryn Roberta. Haran, to. David Daff, son of John
| Duff, of 479 County street. No date for the wedding has been set.

Miss Haran was graduated from the New Bedford High School in June,
1917, and from Fitehburg Normal School in 1919. She is a member of the
faculty of the John B. deValles School. Mr, Duff attended the Newman
School in New Jersey and the New Bedford High School and is now engaged
in the coal business. «

OUT FOR COUNCIL

A YOUNG NEW BEDFORD MATRON | HARBOR’S LATEST—T

JOHN DUFF
~ 22

vi

Tugboat John Duff is the recent acquisition ‘of the New Bedford Tow
Boat Company which has been added to the facilities offered shipping at this
port. The new tugboat, acquired from the surplus supplies of the Navy De
partment, was built by the Chester Shipbuilding Company of Philadelphia, It
Was registered. and in the service of the War Department as the Ghester,
name which has been changed to John Duff for the senior. member,of thesfimn
of David Duk & Son’

The boat is bigger and more powerful than either of the other tugboats,
the S. C, Hart’and the d. -T. Sherman, now operated by the company, - It has
been acquired because, of the demand for a more powerful tug in handling the
bigger steamers now running to the port here, The John Duff’is 79 feet long,
21.6 fect beam, ard 98 gross tons.. It can develop 35¢ ltorse-power, 100 horse-
power in excess of that developed by the J. T. Sherman.

The boat was ‘built m. 1918 at Delanco, N. J., and was in the service of
the War Department during the war. Since the war, the tugboat has been
laid up together with the other surplus vessels operated by the Government

during the war. The boat has been out of commission during the last two
years.

The picture shows a view of the Nauset Ware-
house, which has just completed the erection of two
large additional units to its storage plant on Nauset
street at the head of Reynolds street. Unit No. 4
which is one story high (21 feet inside height) meas-
ures 100 feet on two sides, 96 feet on a third side and
70 feet on the fourth side. It has a capacity of 4800
bales. Section No. 5 is three stories in height and is
designed to house small lots. It measures 50 feet on
one side, 54%4 feet on another, 70 feet on another and
59 feet on another and will store 1200 bales. Storage
of bales in Section 5 is to be on end so that the in-
dividual bales will be more easily accessible, whereas
_the bales in the other four sections of the warehouse

of the New England Committee on

able record as commander of Malden
post and also as First Vice Command-
er of the State department He took
charge of Malden post with’ a amem-
bership of 67 and left it with a mem-
bership of more than 1,300, He was
an ensign in the Navy during the
war and, if elected, will be the first
naval man to be commander of the
State department

After an illness -of several years,
Adoniram -S. Negus, chief engineer at
the pumping station since 1886, died
at his home -in. East Freetown about
three this morning. He was 72, Mr.
Negus had been seriously’ ill’ for the
past few days, suffering from kidney
trouble Whieh was. the cause of his
lingering illness.

Mr. Negus was one of the veterans
of the New. Bedford water, depart-

ment. He served as ehief engineer
runes

Joseph P, Ramalhete, 62, watch-
man, died early yesterday morning at
his residence, 102 Newton. street. He
leaves a widow, Mrs. Iminia Ramal-
hete; two sons, Abram. Ramalhete, 4
carpenter of 147 Rockland street,
Manuel Ramalhete, living at home;
and four daughters, Miss Lillian
Ramalhete,- living. at |.102. Newton
street, Mrs. Minnie Marshall and
Mrs. Julia Kontes, of this: eity, and
Mrs. Mary _Ramalhete of Fall River.
Besides these members of his imme-
diate family, he is survived by 4
brother, Joseph Ramalhete, and two
sisters.

The deceased was an active mem-
ber of the Monte Pio Society.

Funeral services will be held from
102. Newton street to-morrow morn-
ing, with high mass of requiem at St.
John the Baptist Chhrch at 9 A. M.

|

{Interment will be at the Allen street

cemetery.

_ Assets of This Girl M: Market Manager

\Five Years Ago’ Cecilia Ku-
biak Was Buying Meats
for Father

Now She. Is Real Business|
Head of Two Stores
in: City

Five years ago, when little Cecelia
lkubiak first alighted from her bicycle
to buy meat in the wholesale district
for her father’s market, the business
men near the water front thought it
i/just one more instance of a child being
sent on an errand that would have
been better attended to by a grownup.
|They solemnly filled her orders, carry-
ing out huge sides of meat to the
basket attached to her bicyele, As she
pedaled out of sight they chucked to
each other over the “big butcher,” as
ithey called the girl of 14, who was un-
lusually slight and small for her age.
The dealers in the wholesale dis-
|trict no longer chuckle over Miss
\ixubiak, even behind her back, for
she’s a grown-up young lady now, 4
customer of five years’ standing, and
as they all know, the competent man-
Jager of two thriving markets on
Kempton street. She doesn't come
| down on her bicycle any more; she
| whizzes through Union street in a
Ford truck, for it takes more than a
bicycle basket to carry back the sup-
plies she can’t wait to have delivered.
Yes, this is a serious story; rosy, blue-
eyed little Cecelia Kubiak, who hasn't
lyet rounded out her 19th year, is a

real business woman.

' Shipwreck in an open boat in the!

but one of the endless chain of ad-
Ventures whose reminiscence links a)
New Bedford woman to the Pacific)
scenes in which she spent ten years of}
her life as a teacher of Philippine}
youth. ‘ |
Teacher, in the largest sense. In-|
struction six days a week in subjects |
ranging from geography to embroid- |
@ry in three intermediate grades in
Subic may have been the vocation of
Mrs. Charlotte D. Bonner, of 359
‘orth street, from 1993 to 1914. But
hile her husband Fred D. Bonner,
;: ted as supervisor, teacher, member
w= the Board of Health,, Civil Service
sxaminer and Inspecter of Public
“operty, she found such interesting
yOeations as teaching summers. in
ne Insular Normal School, organizing
tirls’ athletics, introducing domestic
Science into native home life, and}
heoOinge out midnights to assist the|
‘American doctor. |
’ Adventures Began Soon.
Teachér! Missionary rather, or
pionwr. For the publie school sys-
tem -of the Philippines evolved under
the experiments of American teachers
pioneering in the opening years of the
century. All of them had to manu-
facture their own tools, design their |
Own methods, almost create their own
aims in teaching. Not all missionaries,
craftsmen, and educational psycholo-
gists, however, have had the interest-
ing experiences of this New~ Bedford

woman.
“As a matter of fact,’’ smiled Mrs.
Bonner, “adventure began to

f transport-‘McClellan’ which took us
Over was still in the New York docks,
word came that the horses which
grove us through a February blizzard
had frozen to death on the way back!”

Graduating from Yale in 1901, Mr. |
Bonner was ifhmediately named, and }
with a group of 20 sailed from San
Francisco in September.

| TOBE OR fe Ue

The Bassindale farm, at Middle street, Acushnet, is again af the dis
posal of any individual or organivation wishing to give a day’s outing te city
youngsters whose play is restricted to hot city streets and back yards, Mis,
Mary Bassindale, who with fer husband, Thomas M. Bassindale, and their
daughter, Miss Evelyn, Jast summer played hosts to 50 wards of the Family
Welfare Society ror a memorable picnic, repeats the invitation she gave then.

Mrs. Bassindale writes The Standard, ‘‘I would like to offer the use of
my farm to any lady or, gentleman or any society that would like to give the
poor children of the city a day in the country, and I will give my services
gladly to help make their day one to be remembered. I don’t care how many
children or how many societies take up this offer.’’

Last year public spirited citizens volunteered trucks to take the boys and
girls out to the Bassindale farm, They had the run of a beautiful pine grave,
from which Mr. Bassindale cleared the brush especially for the occasion, and
scampered over the’ fields and through the shady orchard to heart’s content,
The big barn was open to them in event of rain. Mrs, Bassindale and her
daughter served a fine, wholesome country dinner to the whole outfit. Who'll
start the ball rolling for this year’s outing, now thatthe place is provided?

The New Bedford Communi
School of Religious Education wil
open for the fourth term on Tuesday
Oct. 3,, As in former years it is to by
condifcted under the auspices of thi
|New Bedford District Sunday Schoo
Association, and it will*be held in the
Y, M. C. A., meeting one evening each
week. The first term will continué
from October through December, and]
the second term from January}
through March.

Walter J. Homan, the newly elected i
director of the school, is a graduatey
of Penn College, Oskaloosa; Toway
where he majored in religious educa- |
tion, received the degree of Bachelor
of Arts’ and a first grade teacher's

Map indicating proposed extension of Spring street (as marked by dotted line) to Front street which has been Tepu-
diated by Mayor Remington as too expensive. It was proposed to establish a public market, in the block between Spring
street extended and Commercial street to relieve the jam in Union street mornings. The Mayor suggests that the city land
(marked in the plan above by X) be used instead as equally available and less burdensome to the taxpayer ’s pocketbook,

Charles Mitchell, frhose real name
in Greek is Charalampos S. Mitsialis;
and who formerly conducted two shoe
repairing establishments at thé North}
End, oné at 350 Coggeshall street,
and the other at 1947 Purchase street,
writes to friends here of present-day
life in Greece. In his letter he asks
that it be printed in The Standard

Charles Mitchell.

that all of his countrymen may read
it.

Mr. Mitsialis has only recently been
released from service with the Greek
ormy ‘and he is now living with his
wife and mother on'the island of P
os, one.of a group of seven Greek is-
lands in the Ionian sea It is of in-
terest that olive oil from this island
won the first prize.at the Paris exposi-

NEW WONDER LOOM TO

John Barrett, }

The New Bedford Woman's Club}
brings;to New Bedford this evenine|}
a speaker of the first magnitude, the
Hon. John Barrett, diplomat and
statesman, Mr. Barrett will speak at
eight in the First Baptist Church on
“International Ameria,’ a subject
which through his service as United! q
States Minister to Argentina, Panama
and Colombia, and his 13 years a
| Director General of ‘the Pan Ameri-
ean Union, is one no other Americay
ts better qualified to handle. Local
pride, too, based on the fact. that
Mr. Barrett is by birth a New Eng-
lander, a native of! Vermont and a
graduate of Dartmouth College, will
assume Mr. Barrett a welcome.

The Pan-American 3uilding in |

|

Washington, considered the most}
beautiful in the national capital and
chosen ‘as; the meeting place of the
recent’ Armaments conference, is one
monument to Mr. Barrett's energy ana
vision. It was designed and built un-
der his direction whilé he was Di-
rector General of the Pan American
Union. i

Mr.’ Barrett has given in all 27
years to the official service of his
country. He first won distinction in
1894-1898 as U. S. Minister to Siam
and served later in other’ Asiatic and

Dominique J. Jarry, prominent img
the French colony in the North End}
and one of the most influential cite)
izens in that part of the city, died at
his home, 109 Ashley Boule last
evening about ter... He w |

Born in West Farnham, Canada, in

this city 37 years ago. Within a few
years after his§ arrival in this city)
he became proprietor of a bakery,
jand about three years ago incorporat-
ed the Mayflower Bakery of which he
was treasurer and thé heaviest stock-
holder. |

About the middle of August MrJ

Jarry made a trip to Canada by autos}
mobile, for his health. He |
}

no benefit from his month’s vacation
there and upon his rettirn” imme-

Dominique. J. Jarry.

|
|

diately took to bed, where he has
been confined the past. two weeks. Mr:
Jarry suffered from stomach trouble.

He was afliliated with many organ-y
izations in the city. He was a mem-
bér of tle Federation of Franco-
Americans, Sharpshooters, No. 1, of
the North End, the French Chamber
of Commerce, Board of Trade, Néw
;Bedford Lodge. of Elks, Nov 73, Tre-
mont Club, and’ president of ‘the
| Twenty-five Association, a loan com-
pany, and a mémber and director of
the Master Bakers' Association, In
1916 Mr. Jarry was a candidate for
alderman from ward two. Mr. Jare
ry was a great believer in education
: ; : and was paying for the education. in
ae : = ee {college and’ convent of half a dozen
boys and girls,

winner of the complete. artist’s outfit}

offered by the New Bedford Masonic
Club for the best poster submitted by
a boy or a girl announcing the com-
ing here Sunday evening of the
Rhondda Welsh Male Glee Singers.

James’s poster was a large affair,
painted on an orange back ground.
At the bottom is an excellent painting]
of a soloist standing by a grand piano
at which the pianist is seated, both
being. carefully} lighted by a Rear
lamp. The poster is exhibited at the
New Bedford | Theate this week,
where the ‘Glee Singers will appear
|Sunday night.

In the picture above are some of the outstanding figures at the Booth
Mill picnic at Fort “Phoenix yesterday. Left to right, they are: Fred W.
Steele, treasurer of the Booth Mill, Mary Beaur, winner of the 100-yard dash
at the Young Women’s Christian Association meet at Boston and one of the
crack forwards on the Booth girls’ indoor soccer team, and Clarence E. Benson,
superintendent of the mill. In the right hand corner is Helen Beaur, who plays
forward on the soeecer team with her sister. In the lower picture is a group
of happy Booth bathing beauties and their friends.. Left to right in the front
row are Fanny Conley, Emily Legere, Elizabeth Slater, Eleanor Winterbottom,
Irene Thuette, Charles Winterbottom, in the back row, David Sher, Edward
Slater, Edward Rapposa and Harry Spence.

Above, left—Staircase and corner of reception hall. Richly onhelatone Co a and luxurious carpeting relieve the

austerity of the black and white marble floor and (plain walls. Above, right-"he dining room is the stateliest room
in the house. The screen in the corner would touch the, ceiling of the ordinary cottage dining room. Dull gold predom-
inates in the color scheme. Below, left—A large toom, but small by compari on with the others, the library has an air
of intimate comfort. Jacobean furniture and hangings im a rich design of b: and foliage combine effectively. Below,
right—The reception hall affords a vista into the library and living room) t is lighted by three tall French doors
opening on the semi-circular terrace at the front @ the house. Insert—Entyance front of Colonel Green’s mansion.

Mrs. Martha Smith Doesn't
Want to “Chase Time
All Day”’

Mattapoisett Woman Still
Keeps House, Sews

and Reads

Ninety-four years old today is Mrs.
Martha Smith, of Mattapoisett. So
well has she learned the lesson of
g time in her nine decades of life that

3 a) she. is up at 5:30 every morning, rain
faa or shine, winter or summer.

“Land, if I didn’t get up in the
morning, I'd be chasing time all day.”

§ That's why she can’t afford to be lazy
in the morning, she says.

Neither is she lazy at any time in the
\day, despite the fact that she is the

majoldest woman in Maitapoisett. Living
with her son on the Fairhaven road,
Ashe has entire care of her house, and
finds time besides to sew and keep
abreast of the times by reading the

Mrs. Martha Smith,

sinagazines and newspapers every day.
@yonce a fortnight a woman Gomés to
clean, and the “hired man’’ does the

#heavy labor about the house.
Today she finished up her house-
work early, to be ready and fresh this
Bsevening for a little informal dinner
Man with a few old friends at the New Bed=
)ford Hotel in celebration of her birth=

Mrs. Mabelle Bassett, who. won over Eliot H. Wefer in yesterday’s contest ta day. pas ene

represent the Third Bristol Distri¢t on the Republican. State. Committee. 1 iy qs Usually Smiling. ;

2 She is smiling. in the photograph,
and she is usually smiling all the time
for she feels that she has “many,
many blessings,”. and her “troubles
are mostly behind her.’ She is well)
and véry alert mentally. She doesn’t
even wear glasses all the time.

She feels sorry for others in this

the News

Young Brother Couldn’t See

Freckles Contest

“Tag! You're it.” ‘ {

Little Miss Alice Burke’s blue eyes
widened under their thick, dark lashes,
and the apple blossom pink of her’
cheek deepened. Standing at ithe:
counter in The Standard business of
fice yesterday transacting some bus
ness for her employer, she had come,
unexpectedly face to face with the rn
porter who was just setting out to find
her and announce the great news.

“No, not really? Do you mean I a’

really to be Miss New Bedford?” she BM
exclaimed in the first moment of he :
happy astonishment. It was true she |
jhad to believe it, even though shé ha
wished Sunday the judges would an-
nounce their decision immediately ZI
she'could go’right up-and congratulate
the winner and the suspensé w a be
ended. It was she, Alice Burke,

self, whom the five judges: had

| consideration, out of 117 girls, t
oe proud title of Miss New Bea edfor

Dennis A. Taylor and Martip if A

4 Taylor, sons of Mr. and Mrs. John H.

Taylor, of 50 Princeton street, both
lost their lives while fighting in

Taylor, (left) and Martin| —
J. Taylor (right), |

France. There is now a movement
on foot to name the square at Acush-)

or square in their honor. The square] 4
e at the south entrane- to Brook-
@lawn park. : ‘
Martin was with the American Ex-] |
peditionary Forces, and died at Mo-
bile* Hospital No. 5 following wounds
ahe received while bearing a message
mito his commanding officer at the
Seol\yerdun front, The date of his death }
was Oct. 18, 1918 , {
Dennis was with the Canadian
orces, serving with. the McLean
kilties, 46th Battal.on. He was killed
outright Sept. 2, 1918, Martin was 18]
t the time of his death and Dennis)
was 31. “
Both young men were brought up
n the North End. Martin, prior to
stment, worked as a plumber’s!
lper and Dennis was a mill worker. |
The movement that is being made to
perpetuate the memory of the young)
men is sponsored by Haworth Tay-
jlor, a cousin. The matter is now in
the hands of the special City Council
Committee on Memorials. »

Scenes taken during practice drills by New Bedford firemen in preparation for th 3 i BH 4
Standard flag. The snapshots were made while the firemen were Seitieg Weel aftertioos. ou Laer est ne ae 3
Maxfield. The top view shows the men of Engine Company No. 3 fastening the hose to a hydrant. Two men have ae
to the rear to unwind the hose from the truck. The picture at the lower left shows men of the same compan ‘ bh
ing the hose. At lower right, Assistant Fire Chicf Frederick E. Ricketson, is explaining the workin; $s e A jue in
to a group of men from Engine No. 2, Headquarters company. | ie iss. hey. iY

Those who have seen the Clarence A. Cook school om Summer. street on’
the site of the old High school have remarked that it is one of the finest of
the newer schools in the city. It is a building that has numerous features
that make it distinct among the school buildings of the city, The auditorium
includes more wood panelling than is in any other of the newer schools, and
used in the gallery front, it sets that off to, advantage. |

The exterior of the building is attractive, with exits on all sides. There
is a recessed front on Summer street. Plans for the building were by Nat
|G, Smith, and it was erected by the J. W. Bishop Company.” The pictures
show the front of the auditorium and. ‘stage, together with one of the doors
| to one of the corridars.

Rey. Nathaniel William Mat-
thews Died Suddenly in
Lowell Last Evening

Was First Pastor of First P.
M. Here---Founded
Second P. M.

Rev. Nathaniel William. Matthews,
widely remembered in New Bedford
as the first pastor of the First Primi-
tive Methodist Church, and founder
of the Second Primitive Methodist
chureh in this city, died suddenly
Thursday evening in Lowell. An at-
tack of heart trouble, coming after an

of but a few days, led to his

Sie. Sal Se i fs totes Aes

Picture in the upper left hand corner shows Mrs. George Geno of Proctor,
Vt., who won a prize for coming the longest distance to the reunion of the
Predette family yesterday at the Dartmouth Inn, North Dartmouth.
She travelled 220 miles to get to the reunion, In the upper right a group
of the Fredettes of the ‘‘rising generation’’ are shown, In the lower left,
we see William George Fredette, Jr., of 213 Brownell street, this city. He
was the youngest of all the Fredettes, having only a three weeks’ seeing
acquaintanceship with this world. Next are the oldest man and the oldest
woman at the reunion. They are Fred Fredette and Phoebe Fredette. In
the lower right is Master Norman Fredette of Nash road, this city, who
seems perfectly content eating an ice cream cone.

torate at tne Kirst Primitive Methodist
Church of New Bedford, from 1889 to
1294, cut short only by the five year
limit rule then enforced in the. de-
nomination. While pastor of the Weld
street church, he organized the Sec-
ond Primitive Church in this city, on
Ccunty street.

From this city, Rev. Mr. Mat-
thews went to Fall River, as pastor
from 1894 to 1897, of the Second
Primitive Methodist Church in that
|city. Since 1897, he has served con-
tinuously in Lowell, where he went
first after leaving his initial charge in
Trenton, N. J. It was in 1879 that Rev.
N. W. Matthews was sent to Lowell to
organize the First Primitive Methodist
Church in that city. After a stay of
four years there, he returned to. Penn-
sylvania, where he served the Primi-
tive Church in St. Clair from 1883 to
1885, and in Plymouth from 1885 10
1889. He came from Plymouth to
New Bedford.

NEW OFFICIAL

TAKES CHARGE

Henry C. Falby Succeeds Ed-
ward P, Haskell as Dep-
uty Port Collector

Promises Business Men of
City Co-operation in Up-
building Trade

Henry C. Falby, who was appoint-
ed early this week by the collector of
the Port of Boston, W. W. Lufkin, to
the position of deputy collector of the
Port of New Bedford, arrived here
yesterday morning to take charge of
the office here. Mr. Falby's appoint-
ment was made to fil] the vacancy
caused by the death of Edward P.
Haskell.

Mr. Falby comes to this city with
25 years of customs work experience
in the Boston district. He entered
the employ of the government there
as @ messenger and worked up
through the grades in the department
to inspector, first class, which was his
rating prior to his appointment aq
deputy collector of this port. Inspec-
tor E. B. Henry who has been on ;
duty here for some time aiding Assist-
ant Deputy Customs. Collector Arthur
E. Duffy, it was said yesterday, will
remain on duty here for some timé,
until Mr. Falby becomes familiar with

the. .duties.here.

Mr. Falby gave out the following
statement:

“I beg to announce through the
courtesy of your press that at the re-
quest of the Hon. W. W. Lufkin, Col-
lector of Customs for the district of

James R, Feeley, 26, of-140° State,
street, died shortly after 711 lastnight
at St. Luke's Hospital asia Yesult of]
internal injuries received at 40:30 i ;
front of the Postoffice building whea }
he was run. 6ver by a heavy truek

owned by George ae Cobb; 2172

aign conducted by John F. Hateh,

Was riding on the truck owned

y Mr Cobb and according to* memi-
bers. of his family fell from it when
he attempted to pass red fire to per-
sons on a pas: = machine. i

Bystanders on Pleasant street]
rushed to his assistance.and with the
aid-of them he walked to the Post-
office Pharmacy of which his brother,
William Weeley, is the manager, Dr.}
Thomas B, Horan, 204 Elm street. |
and Dr. Denis J.. Lowney, 72° Woster
street, who were in the drug store
at the time, examined Mr, Meeley and |
then ordered his removal to
Luke's Hospital. Shortly -after ar
viving there he began to weaken rap-
idly and died less than an hour after
the accident occurred.

Mr. Feeley was well known
throughout the ‘center of the city.
He was born on Pleasant street, just
north of ‘the Common and lived in
that vicinity all his lifes Te was a
sraduate of St. Joseph's parochial
{school and-up to a short time ago
had been employed as a lineman by
the New England Telephone and Tel-
jegsraph Company. Mr. Feeley served
in the army during the World War
{and was stationed for eight. months at
Camp Jackson, South Carolina.

Mr. Feeley is survived by#-his pars
ents, Mr. and .Mrs. Edward J. Rees
ley, four brother William, award)
Cornelius and Frank and~ two sis-f

‘ON DUTY AT SUPERIOR COURT SESSION HERE ~
ON DUTY AT SUPERIOR COURT SESSION

Here they are, Sheriff Isaac E. Willetts and five of the New Bedford
deputy sheriffs, the officers usually selected by the Sheriff to maintain order
and take care of the juries at the civil sessions of Superior Court in New
Bedford. The veteran of them all in this picture is Deputy Sheriff Walter R,
Spooner, the second from the left. Deputy Spooner’s recollections of service
as a county officer go back more than forty years. In fact, Mr. Spooner is
the veteran of them all in Bristol county. He was appointed a deputy Jan.
1, 1877, 45 years ago, and he has been on the job ever since. You can guess
Mr, Spooner’s present age from the fact that he was 29 when he was ap-
pointed,

Another veteran in the group here is Deputy Edmund J. Whalley, second
from the right, but while a veteran in years of service he’s only a youngster
compared to the years Mr. Spooner has been on the job. On the extreme left
is Deputy Joseph ©. Patnaude, another officer who has served for many years,
On Sheriff Willetts’s right, standing next to Deputy Spooner, is Deputy Henry
W. Mason, court crier. His face is familiar through his long period of ser-
vice as Chief of Police. Though Deputy Mason has passed the four score
mark he carries himself with that military carriage that he acquired as a
cavalry officer during the Civil War. The two youngest men in the picture
are Sheriff Willetts, the big man in the center and Deputy J. Arthur Balthazar,
who is the sheriff’s right hand» man in executing his orders in this section of
the county. The Sheriff looks every inch a sheriff, Deputy Balthazar is on
the extreme right in this picture.

Mrs. Caleb Anthony Hayward, Jr., whose marriage Oct. 14 touok place
at the home of her parents, Mr. and Mrs. Henry P. Burt, 355 Union street.
The ceremony was characterized by beauty and simplicity. The bride, who
was Miss Beatrice Milliken Burt, looked very lovely in a quaint replica of her
grandmother’s wedding gown and an exquisite real lace veil. Mr. and Mrs,
Hayward will be at home temporarily with Mr. and Mrs. Burt upon their re-
turn from their wedding trip. :

Superintendent of Parks Everett B. Sherman is mighty proud of the
splendid appearance of the greenhouse at Buttonwood Park. This greenhouse,
which was built last year is used for raising plants for the parks throughout
the city and Mr. Sherman says that he expects that the park flower displays
will be unusually good next year. Péter C. Keith is the park gardener.

The upper picture shows the exterior of the greenhouse.’ Mr. Keith
is shown at the left of the center picture while at the right is an interior
view of the greenhouse. The picture at the’ bottom shows the new barn |
which has just been compVeiegees. Buttonwood Park ata cost of $4,500, |

With these teachers, going tu Sunday school is a pleasant task for the children at St. Andrew’s Episcopal Church, Belleville road. There are more than
50 teachers‘in the school, and the attendance is remarkably high, ehiefly because, as the children say, ‘*they have nice teachers,’

Gregson, Gladys Turner, Edith Etchells and Theresa Lamb, Third row, left to right: Frank Holden, Mary Doolan, Mildred Perkins, Mrs. Robert Quirk, Mrs,
Richard Russell, Gladys Ainsworth, Sarah Dean, Ethel Ridgway, Phyllis Cassidy and Mrs. Emma Horton. Rear row, left to right: Winifred Kent, Byvelyi

The accompanying sketch drawn by Thomas B, Syme of Boston, shows the new Presbyterian church which will
replace the present one at County and Kempton streets.
The exterior, a simple but dignified treatment of the colonial style, will be of water struck brick with
trimmings. The front of the building faces County street and the two entrances give access to :
of seating 400 persons. From the vestibule a stairway leads to a small gallery which seats another hundred people.
Directly under the entrance on Kempton street is the pastor’s room from’ which a door opens directly. to: the
pulpit platform. On the opposite side of the platform is the coir room, the choir and org

recess behind the pulpit. In the basement) is located a large social and Sunday School h
oom, primary room and ladies’ parlor. At the rear end of the bagement

Representatives of New interests and the Internal —
Revenue Bureau and friends of Harry Hathaway were present this morning
to hear the announcement by Maleolm . Nichols, Collector of the Massa-
chusetts district, that New Bedford has been designated a division head-
quarters, and see Mr. Hathaway sworn into office as division chief.

The opning reception of the Mt. Pleasant Community Center took place “
at the Mt. Pleasant school last night, attended by more than a thousand men
and women, and nlivend by music and dancing. Mayor Walter H. B. Reming-

ton, Superintendent of Schools and Mrs, True C. Morrill, Assistant Superinten-
dent Clarence B, Howell, and the members of the School Committee were among

the invited guests.

Music for the dancing was rendred by the’ Universal Orchstra, which vol-
unteered its services. The palms and flowers which decorated the school
were donated by H. V. Sowl and James W. Wooler. The commattee in charg
of the ntertainment was Herman Mreer, chairman, Stephen Bond, Ed. Le-
gault, Perey Mellor, John Downey, Hiss Audrey Belyea, Miss Louise LaRoche,
Mrs, Mary Phelan, Miss Coates.

The Woman’s Club of th Community Center elected the following officers
last night, President, Mrs. Thomas Holdn; vice prsident, Mrs. Naney Booker;
reording secrtary, Mrs. Alice Mrcer; financial scretary, Mrs. Yates; treasur-
er, Mrs. Ina Soul. The club will meet Tusday venings at 7:30 in the Mt.
Pleasant School building. Next Tusday it will hold a whist party to raise

funds for its work.

John Richard Barbour.

This model full-rigged ship is the product of the spare time of Captain
W. J. Collins of the Great Round Shoals lightship and a.seaman vigger, Miguel
Monteira. After they have finished their stretch of duty°on the lig itship they
turn to.and produce valuable models like this. Captain Collins can turn out
the woodwork, huil and spars and small boats and paint it in about a month.
Monteiro does the rigging and it takes him about two week The hull is
made from pine and the spars from cherry taken from the discarded desks of
the little schoolhouse at South Yarmouth’ where Captain Cothins received his
education, Monteiro is the man at the left and Captain-Colling is at the right,

Andrew Es TWathaway is 75 years old today. lis Jong life has been
packed to the brim-with varied activities. He first attained distinction when,
{as & boy of 14, -he shared with another lad the honor of raising the flag
above the Fifth Street Grammar School. In later years his extensive -ventures
as a theatrical magnate have been uppermost in the public mind. Mr. Hath-
away lives at 120 Mill street. The picture on the right shows Mr, Hathaway
at thé age of 12,

HER SONG BOOK
#3 1S ANIMATED

4 Rae Les
Mrs, A. H. Andrews Is Au-
thoress of Interesting
One Volume Edition

Delightful Verse
Traces the History of
American Music

In

She

“When I was a little girl in school
and ought to have~been busy at some-
thing else, 1 wa generally making
up?verses, and the little girl in the
next desk was drawing pictures to go}
with them,” confessed Mrs = = |
Andrews last We “Perhaps
that was the real beginning of The
Animated Song Book.” |

The latest number of Federation
Topics, a woman’s club magazine,
contains the annoetincement that Mrs.
Susie: L, Andrews is giving readings
this winter from her Animated Song
Book, illustrated by Mrs. Lelah Mac-
Pherson: Parker, of Taunton, and
Miss Dorothy BH. Beaton,-of Brockton
Mrs. Andréws revealed’ many gifts

durin her recent termas president of
the New Bedford Woman's Club, but
her friends did not suspect her of be-
fing an authoress. So an investigation

Was immediately in order. It/ re-
Vealed that Mrs. Andrews and her
collbaorators have worked out a

charming entertainment program out-
lining the progress of American mnu-
gic, in a form which justifies the title
of an animated song book,

A Idmited Edition.

The edition of the Animated Song
Book is limited to one volume, so
that It is sure to be of interest to
collectors of rare’ beoks. Only one
yolume, but that a big one. It is
something more than six feet tall and
wide in proportion... The pages con-

tain reading matter, musical themes,
and illustrations, but the best part

Miss Dorothy E. Beaton.

of the text reposes in the brain of
Mrs. Andrews.

The book limits itself strietly to
music composed in America by Amer-
ican composers— a fact which made
Mrs. Andrews’s search for illustrative
material to accompany the four per-
iods. of which the book tells, more
difficult than one would think. So
many ‘American’ composers
born in England or elsewhere. With
the first period, indian music, this dif-
ficulty was not encountered. The In-
dians and their tribal composers
were 100 per cent American. The
music of the Pilgrims led directly in-
to the hymnal, which -contains the
work of composers of many lands.
The “Old Fashioned Music’ period,
ranging roughly from the Revolution
to the Civil War, was surprisingly poor
n real American products ouside of

were |

divine

Mrs A. H. Andrews.

Only in the modern
period, the fourth in’ the book, did
Mrs. Andrews and her collaborators
# find themselves embarrassed more hy
the wealth of choice than the difticul-
ty of finding something suitable and
i truly American.

plantation songs.

In Clever Verse.

Mrs. Andrews tells in clever verse,

which never .sacrifices sense (for

te rhyme, the purpose for which the

- book was “‘written’. She explains al-

/.oist

i

|American music and the content of
jthe illustrative songs and dances. As
jshe swings open the great black cov-
lers of the: book, “dedicated to the
best in American music,” Mrs. Parker
and Miss Beaton emerge in the cos-
|tume of the period. ‘The two are both
|pianists, and accompany each other,
|but Mrs. Parker is above all the singer
and Miss Beaton the dancer. In au-
lthentic Indian robes, in demure Pil-
grim garb, in frilly old “fashioned
|frocks worn with century-old boen-
|nets, and finally in the brilliant even-
ing gowns of the present the two “‘il-
|lustrators, make — lovely pictures

jagainst the tall white pages and black

lettering of the gigantic book.

Mrs. Andrews is characteristically
modest about her part in evolving
the “Animated Song Book,” although

|she.admits she did not get the idea

|from anything she had seen before:

|
Her

interest—non-professional en-

|tirely, for she is not a musician—in

|

|

American music, and her admiration
for the personality and beautiful col-

NE :
Mrs. Lelah MacPherson Parker

oratura soprano voices of her friend,
Mrs. Parker, impelled her to the work,
“Tt is the first thing of the’ kind f
have ever done,” said Mrs. Andrews,
“the first serious application of my?
natural inclination te put things into
rhyme. If the American musie yol-
ume of the song book takes well, it
may perhaps be followed by similar
sketches of music of other nations.” }
Consumed Several Months, |
Work on the Animated Song Book
consumed several months. First came
the search for basic facts and mater-
ial, then the selection, Next Mrs.
Andrews composed and memorized her

| verses, Mrs. Parker studied the songs,

Miss Beaton evolved the Indian
dances, the spontaneous whirling about
of a Pilgrim lass when her elders
weren’t looking, old fashioned dances,
and the most modern solo éffects. Mrs.
Parker selected the layout of the
pages of the book, which were ex-
ecuted in enlargement by @ profes-
sional letterer and désigner.

Mrs. Parker, who went to Taunton
from her home in Oklahoma, has vis-
ited New Bedford on several occa-
|sions, and her friends here remember
| with pleasure her cultivated and beau-
tiful voice. Miss Beaton, Mrs. An-
drews's niece, has been a& student of
esthetic dancing from early childhood.
The trio opened the pages of their
song book. to the public for the first
time two weeks ago before the Wom
jan’s Club of Hudson, They are to
appear next before the’ Matrons @Jub
of Brockton. - The New Bedford Wom-
jan's Club is looking forward to wit-
nessing the achievement of its past
| president some time in December.

VETERAN
OF CIVIL WAR

__ Kee 15> 1922

William C. Gifford, a Na-
tive of Mattapoisett, Died
at Home of Daughter Here
This Morning

William C. Gifford, Civil
eran and for more than
resident of this section, died at the
residence of his daughter, Mrs. Harry
Yates, at Maxfield street, at 8:15
yesterday morning following an ill-
ness which for eight weeks confined
him to his bed. Mr. Gifford had been
in feeble health for many years.

Deceased leaves two daughters, Miss
Etta Gifford and Mrs, Harry Yates,

War vet-
years a

75

75

William C. Gifford,

both of 75 Maxfield street, a four
Sons, Francis, a blacksmith in this city,
Joseph Thomas, of North Main street,
and Elmer C,, of 30 Water street,
Fairhaven, and Albert E., of Wore
ter.

Mr. Gifford at the of 18 left a
Whaler out of Mattapoisett to enlist is
the Civil War. At the expiration of
hine moaths’ first period of ene
listment, he re-enlisted June 1863,
in Company I, of the Third Regiment
Massachusetts Volunteer Infantry
with which he served until the enu

the war.

When peace was declared, without
waiting for the mustering out his
company, he came north, and enlisted
jin the navy. For five atter
the close of the Civil War he Was fol-
lowing’ the sea, When he returned
to this section it was to make his
home at 23 Water street, Pairhaven,
where he lived for 34 years,. He was
a member of Post One of the G. A.
R.

Two years ago he came to live with
his daughter, Mrs. Harry Yates, at
whose home he died.

Mr. Gifford was born in- Matta-
poisett, in 1844. It was upon a whaler
from that town that he made his first
voyage at the age of 14. An accident,
nearly fatal, marred it, He was
knocked from the deck of the bark
into the ocean and Was rescued by the
mate.

age

26,

of

years

ONE OF LARGEST MOTOR TRUCK FLEETS SERVING COTTON MILL

-$- e>-—

The picture shows a view of a part of the fleet of Mack seven-ton trucks
owned and operated by the Interstate Trucking Company of New Bedford.
These trucks, together with many more that are hired by, the company, are
kept constantly on the move, carrying freight to and fro between the various
cotton manufacturing and finishing establishments in New England. They
operate solely on long distance work between cities and do little or no local
trucking.
